                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2011 475:7355"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2011-07-10"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2011 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Potato (Solanum tuberosum L.) is the world&#8217;s most important non-grain food crop and is central to global food security. It is clonally propagated, highly heterozygous, autotetraploid, and suffers acute inbreeding depression. Here we use a homozygous doubled-monoploid potato clone to sequence and assemble 86% of the 844-megabase genome. We predict 39,031 protein-coding genes and present evidence for at least two genome duplication events indicative of a palaeopolyploid origin. As the first genome sequence of an asterid, the potato genome reveals 2,642 genes specific to this large angiosperm clade. We also sequenced a heterozygous diploid clone and show that gene presence/absence variants and other potentially deleterious mutations occur frequently and are a likely cause of inbreeding depression. Gene family expansion, tissue-specific expression and recruitment of genes to new pathways contributed to the evolution of tuber development. The potato genome sequence provides a platform for genetic improvement of this vital crop. The genome of the potato (Solanum tuberosum L.), a staple crop vital to food security, has been sequenced. The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium sequenced a homozygous doubled-monoploid potato clone as well as a heterozygous diploid clone. Genome analysis reveals traces of at least two genome duplication events and genes specific to Asterids, a large clade of flowering plants of which the potato is the first to be sequenced. Gene presence/absence variants and other potentially deleterious mutations are frequent and may be the cause of inbreeding depression. The genome sequence will facilitate genetic improvements in the potato with a view to improving yield and to increasing disease and stress resistance of this crop, which is a now a significant component of worldwide food production and is becoming increasingly important in the developing world."/>

        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Nature"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="The genome of the potato (Solanum tuberosum L.), a staple crop vital to food security, has been sequenced. The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium sequenced a homozygous doubled-monoploid potato clone as well as a heterozygous diploid clone. Genome analysis reveals traces of at least two genome duplication events and genes specific to Asterids, a large clade of flowering plants of which the potato is the first to be sequenced. Gene presence/absence variants and other potentially deleterious mutations are frequent and may be the cause of inbreeding depression. The genome sequence will facilitate genetic improvements in the potato with a view to improving yield and to increasing disease and stress resistance of this crop, which is a now a significant component of worldwide food production and is becoming increasingly important in the developing world."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/m685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig1_HTML.jpg"/>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato</h1>
                    <ul class="c-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-author-popup="group-1" href="#group-1">The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium</a></li></ul>

                    

                    <p class="c-article-info-details" data-container-section="info">
                        
    <a data-test="journal-link" href="/nature"><i data-test="journal-title">Nature</i></a>

                        <b data-test="journal-volume"><span class="u-visually-hidden">volume</span> 475</b>, <span class="u-visually-hidden">pages</span><span itemprop="pageStart">189</span>–<span itemprop="pageEnd">195</span>(<span data-test="article-publication-year">2011</span>)<a href="#citeas" class="c-article-info-details__cite-as u-hide-print" data-track="click" data-track-action="cite this article" data-track-label="link">Cite this article</a>
                    </p>

        <ul class="c-article-subject-list">
            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/genome-evolution" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Genome evolution</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/genomic-analysis" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Genomic analysis</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-genetics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant genetics</a></li>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Potato (<i>Solanum tuberosum</i> L.) is the world’s most important non-grain food crop and is central to global food security. It is clonally propagated, highly heterozygous, autotetraploid, and suffers acute inbreeding depression. Here we use a homozygous doubled-monoploid potato clone to sequence and assemble 86% of the 844-megabase genome. We predict 39,031 protein-coding genes and present evidence for at least two genome duplication events indicative of a palaeopolyploid origin. As the first genome sequence of an asterid, the potato genome reveals 2,642 genes specific to this large angiosperm clade. We also sequenced a heterozygous diploid clone and show that gene presence/absence variants and other potentially deleterious mutations occur frequently and are a likely cause of inbreeding depression. Gene family expansion, tissue-specific expression and recruitment of genes to new pathways contributed to the evolution of tuber development. The potato genome sequence provides a platform for genetic improvement of this vital crop.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">
                        
                            
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/nature10158.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Potato (<i>Solanum tuberosum</i> L.) is a member of the Solanaceae, an economically important family that includes tomato, pepper, aubergine (eggplant), petunia and tobacco. Potato belongs to the asterid clade of eudicot plants that represents <span class="stix">∼</span>25% of flowering plant species and from which a complete genome sequence has not yet, to our knowledge, been published. Potato occupies a wide eco-geographical range<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Hijmans, R. J. Global distribution of the potato crop. Am. J. Potato Res. 78, 403–412 (2001)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2052">1</a></sup> and is unique among the major world food crops in producing stolons (underground stems) that under suitable environmental conditions swell to form tubers. Its worldwide importance, especially within the developing world, is growing rapidly, with production in 2009 reaching 330 million tons (<a href="http://www.fao.org">http://www.fao.org</a>). The tubers are a globally important dietary source of starch, protein, antioxidants and vitamins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Burlingame, B., Mouillé, B. &amp; Charrondiére, R. Nutrients, bioactive non-nutrients and anti-nutrients in potatoes. J. Food Compost. Anal. 22, 494–502 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2063">2</a></sup>, serving the plant as both a storage organ and a vegetative propagation system. Despite the importance of tubers, the evolutionary and developmental mechanisms of their initiation and growth remain elusive.</p><p>Outside of its natural range in South America, the cultivated potato is considered to have a narrow genetic base resulting originally from limited germplasm introductions to Europe. Most potato cultivars are autotetraploid (2<i>n</i> = 4<i>x</i> = 48), highly heterozygous, suffer acute inbreeding depression, and are susceptible to many devastating pests and pathogens, as exemplified by the Irish potato famine in the mid-nineteenth century. Together, these attributes present a significant barrier to potato improvement using classical breeding approaches. A challenge to the scientific community is to obtain a genome sequence that will ultimately facilitate advances in breeding.</p><p>To overcome the key issue of heterozygosity and allow us to generate a high-quality draft potato genome sequence, we used a unique homozygous form of potato called a doubled monoploid, derived using classical tissue culture techniques<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Paz, M. M. &amp; Veilleux, R. E. Influence of culture medium and in vitro conditions on shoot regeneration in Solanum phureja monoploids and fertility of regenerated doubled monoploids. Plant Breed. 118, 53–57 (1999)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2079">3</a></sup>. The draft genome sequence from this genotype, <i>S. tuberosum</i> group Phureja DM1-3 516 R44 (hereafter referred to as DM), was used to integrate sequence data from a heterozygous diploid breeding line, <i>S. tuberosum</i> group Tuberosum RH89-039-16 (hereafter referred to as RH). These two genotypes represent a sample of potato genomic diversity; DM with its fingerling (elongated) tubers was derived from a primitive South American cultivar whereas RH more closely resembles commercially cultivated tetraploid potato. The combined data resources, allied to deep transcriptome sequence from both genotypes, allowed us to explore potato genome structure and organization, as well as key aspects of the biology and evolution of this important crop.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Genome assembly and annotation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Genome assembly and annotation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We sequenced the nuclear and organellar genomes of DM using a whole-genome shotgun sequencing (WGS) approach. We generated 96.6 Gb of raw sequence from two next-generation sequencing (NGS) platforms, Illumina Genome Analyser and Roche Pyrosequencing, as well as conventional Sanger sequencing technologies. The genome was assembled using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2097">4</a></sup>, resulting in a final assembly of 727 Mb, of which 93.9% is non-gapped sequence. Ninety per cent of the assembly falls into 443 superscaffolds larger than 349 kb. The 17-nucleotide depth distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>) suggests a genome size of 844 Mb, consistent with estimates from flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208–218 (1991)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2104">5</a></sup>. Our assembly of 727 Mb is 117 Mb less than the estimated genome size. Analysis of the DM scaffolds indicates 62.2% repetitive content in the assembled section of the DM genome, less than the 74.8% estimated from bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) and fosmid end sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 1</a>), indicating that much of the unassembled genome is composed of repetitive sequences.</p><p>We assessed the quality of the WGS assembly through alignment to Sanger-derived phase 2 BAC sequences. In an alignment length of <span class="stix">∼</span>1 Mb (99.4% coverage), no gross assembly errors were detected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Alignment of fosmid and BAC paired-end sequences to the WGS scaffolds revealed limited (≤0.12%) potential misassemblies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Extensive coverage of the potato genome in this assembly was confirmed using available expressed sequence tag (EST) data; 97.1% of 181,558 available Sanger-sequenced <i>S. tuberosum</i> ESTs (&gt;200 bp) were detected. Repetitive sequences account for at least 62.2% of the assembled genome (452.5 Mb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 1</a>) with long terminal repeat retrotransposons comprising the majority of the transposable element classes, representing 29.4% of the genome. In addition, subtelomeric repeats were identified at or near chromosomal ends (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Using a newly constructed genetic map based on 2,603 polymorphic markers in conjunction with other available genetic and physical maps, we genetically anchored 623 Mb (86%) of the assembled genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), and constructed pseudomolecules for each of the 12 chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), which harbour 90.3% of the predicted genes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="
                        The potato genome.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: 
                        <b>The potato genome.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="987"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Ideograms of the 12 pseudochromosomes of potato (in Mb scales). Each of the 12 pachytene chromosomes from DM was digitally aligned with the ideogram (the amount of DNA in each unit of the pachytene chromosomes is not in proportion to the scales of the pseudochromosomes). <b>b</b>, Gene density represented as number of genes per Mb (non-overlapping, window size = 1 Mb). <b>c</b>, Percentage of coverage of repetitive sequences (non-overlapping windows, window size = 1 Mb). <b>d</b>, Transcription state. The transcription level for each gene was estimated by averaging the fragments per kb exon model per million mapped reads (FPKM) from different tissues in non-overlapping 1-Mb windows. <b>e</b>, GC content was estimated by the per cent G+C in 1-Mb non-overlapping windows. <b>f</b>, Distribution of the subtelomeric repeat sequence CL14_cons.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM254">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To aid annotation and address a series of biological questions, we generated 31.5 Gb of RNA-Seq data from 32 DM and 16 RH libraries representing all major tissue types, developmental stages and responses to abiotic and biotic stresses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 4</a>). For annotation, reads were mapped against the DM genome sequence (90.2% of 824,621,408 DM reads and 88.6% of 140,375,647 RH reads) and in combination with <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, protein and EST alignments, we annotated 39,031 protein-coding genes. RNA-Seq data revealed alternative splicing; 9,875 genes (25.3%) encoded two or more isoforms, indicative of more functional variation than represented by the gene set alone. Overall, 87.9% of the gene models were supported by transcript and/or protein similarity with only 12.1% derived solely from <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 5</a>).</p><p>Karyotypes of RH and DM suggested similar heterochromatin content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tang, X. et al. Assignment of genetic linkage maps to diploid Solanum tuberosum pachytene chromosomes by BAC-FISH technology. Chromosome Res. 17, 899–915 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2204">6</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>) with large blocks of heterochromatin located at the pericentromeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). As observed in other plant genomes, there was an inverse relationship between gene density and repetitive sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). However, many predicted genes in heterochromatic regions are expressed, consistent with observations in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Peters, S. A. et al. Solanum lycopersicum cv. Heinz 1706 chromosome 6: distribution and abundance of genes and retrotransposable elements. Plant J. 58, 857–869 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2221">7</a></sup> that genic ‘islands’ are present in the heterochromatic ‘ocean’.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Genome evolution"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Genome evolution</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Potato is the first sequenced genome of an asterid, a clade within eudicots that encompasses nearly 70,000 species characterized by unique morphological, developmental and compositional features<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Albach, D. C., Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Patterns of embryological and biochemical evolution in the Asterids. Syst. Bot. 26, 242–262 (2001)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2233">8</a></sup>. Orthologous clustering of the predicted potato proteome with 11 other green plant genomes revealed 4,479 potato genes in 3,181 families in common (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>); 24,051 potato genes clustered with at least one of the 11 genomes. Filtering against transposable elements and 153 nonasterid and 57 asterid publicly available transcript-sequence data sets yielded 2,642 high-confidence asterid-specific and 3,372 potato-lineage-specific genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>); both sets were enriched for genes of unknown function that had less expression support than the core Viridiplantae genes. Genes encoding transcription factors, self-incompatibility, and defence-related proteins were evident in the asterid-specific gene set (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 7</a>) and presumably contribute to the unique characteristics of asterids.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="
                        Comparative analyses and evolution of the potato genome.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b>Comparative analyses and evolution of the potato genome.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="501"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Clusters of orthologous and paralogous gene families in 12 plant species as identified by OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2264">33</a></sup>. Gene family number is listed in each of the components; the number of genes within the families for all of the species within the component is noted within parentheses. <b>b</b>, Genome duplication in dicot genomes as revealed through 4DTv analyses. <b>c</b>, Syntenic blocks between <i>A. thaliana</i>, potato, and <i>V. vinifera</i> (grape) demonstrating a high degree of conserved gene order between these taxa.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM255">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Structurally, we identified 1,811 syntenic gene blocks involving 10,046 genes in the potato genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 8</a>). On the basis of these pairwise paralogous segments, we calculated an age distribution based on the number of transversions at fourfold degenerate sites (4DTv) for all duplicate pairs. In general, two significant groups of blocks are seen in the potato genome (4DTv <span class="stix">∼</span>0.36 and <span class="stix">∼</span>1.0; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), suggesting two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events. We also identified collinear blocks between potato and three rosid genomes (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>) that also suggest both events (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). The ancient WGD corresponds to the ancestral hexaploidization (γ) event in grape (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), consistent with a previous report based on EST analysis that the two main branches of eudicots, the asterids and rosids, may share the same palaeo-hexaploid duplication event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2322">9</a></sup>. The γ event probably occurred after the divergence between dicots and monocots about 185 ± 55 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2326">10</a></sup>. The recent duplication can therefore be placed at <span class="stix">∼</span>67 million years ago, consistent with the WGD that occurred near the Cretaceous–Tertiary boundary (<span class="stix">∼</span>65 million years ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Fawcett, J. A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous–Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737–5742 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2330">11</a></sup>. The divergence of potato and grape occurred at <span class="stix">∼</span>89 million years ago (4DTv <span class="stix">∼</span>0.48), which is likely to represent the split between the rosids and asterids.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Haplotype diversity"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Haplotype diversity</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>High heterozygosity and inbreeding depression are inherent to potato, a species that predominantly outcrosses and propagates by means of vegetative organs. Indeed, the phenotypes of DM and RH differ, with RH more vigorous than DM (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). To explore the extent of haplotype diversity and possible causes of inbreeding depression, we sequenced and assembled 1,644 RH BAC clones generating 178 Mb of non-redundant sequence from both haplotypes (<span class="stix">∼</span>10% of the RH genome with uneven coverage) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 9–11</a>). After filtering to remove repetitive sequences, we aligned 99 Mb of RH sequence (55%) to the DM genome. These regions were largely collinear with an overall sequence identity of 97.5%, corresponding to one single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) every 40 bp and one insertion/deletion (indel) every 394 bp (average length 12.8 bp). Between the two RH haplotypes, 6.6 Mb of sequence could be aligned with 96.5% identity, corresponding to 1 SNP per 29 bp and 1 indel per 253 bp (average length 10.4 bp).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="
                        Haplotype diversity and inbreeding depression.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: 
                        <b>Haplotype diversity and inbreeding depression.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="419"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Plants and tubers of DM and RH showing that RH has greater vigour. <b>b</b>, Illumina K-mer volume histograms of DM and RH. The volume of K-mers (<i>y</i>-axis) is plotted against the frequency at which they occur (<i>x</i>-axis). The leftmost truncated peaks at low frequency and high volume represent K-mers containing essentially random sequencing errors, whereas the distribution to the right represents proper (putatively error-free) data. In contrast to the single modality of DM, RH exhibits clear bi-modality caused by heterozygosity. <b>c</b>, Genomic distribution of premature stop, frameshift and presence/absence variation mutations contributing to inbreeding depression. The hypothetical RH pseudomolecules were solely inferred from the corresponding DM ones. Owing to the inability to assign heterozygous PS and FS of RH to a definite haplotype, all heterozygous PS and FS were arbitrarily mapped to the left haplotype of RH. <b>d</b>, A zoom-in comparative view of the DM and RH genomes. The left and right alignments are derived from the euchromatic and heterochromatic regions of chromosome 5, respectively. Most of the gene annotations, including PS and RH-specific genes, are supported by transcript data.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM256">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Current algorithms are of limited use in <i>de novo</i> whole-genome assembly or haplotype reconstruction of highly heterozygous genomes such as RH, as shown by K-mer frequency count histograms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 12</a>). To complement the BAC-level comparative analysis and provide a genome-wide perspective of heterozygosity in RH, we mapped 1,118 million whole-genome NGS reads from RH (84× coverage) onto the DM assembly. A total of 457.3 million reads uniquely aligned providing 90.6% (659.1 Mb) coverage. We identified 3.67 million SNPs between DM and one or both haplotypes of RH, with an error rate of 0.91% based on evaluation of RH BAC sequences. We used this data set to explore the possible causes of inbreeding depression by quantifying the occurrence of premature stop, frameshift and presence/absence variants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lai, J. et al. Genome-wide patterns of genetic variation among elite maize inbred lines. Nature Genet. 42, 1027–1030 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2407">12</a></sup>, as these disable gene function and contribute to genetic load (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 13–16</a>). We identified 3,018 SNPs predicted to induce premature stop codons in RH, with 606 homozygous (in both haplotypes) and 2,412 heterozygous. In DM, 940 premature stop codons were identified. In the 2,412 heterozygous RH premature stop codons, 652 were shared with DM and the remaining 1,760 were found in RH only (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Frameshift mutations were identified in 80 loci within RH, 49 homozygous and 31 heterozygous, concentrated in seven genomic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 14</a>). Finally, we identified presence/absence variations for 275 genes; 246 were RH specific (absent in DM) and 29 were DM specific, with 125 and 9 supported by RNA-Seq and/or Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nature Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2427">13</a></sup> annotation for RH and DM, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 15 and 16</a>). Collectively, these data indicate that the complement of homozygous deleterious alleles in DM may be responsible for its reduced level of vigour (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>).</p><p>The divergence between potato haplotypes is similar to that reported between out-crossing maize accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Gore, M. A. et al. A first-generation haplotype map of maize. Science 326, 1115–1117 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2441">14</a></sup> and, coupled with our inability to successfully align 45% of the BAC sequences, intra- and inter-genome diversity seem to be a significant feature of the potato genome. A detailed comparison of the three haplotypes (DM and the two haplotypes of RH) at two genomic regions (334 kb in length) using the RH BAC sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 17 and 18</a>) revealed considerable sequence and structural variation. In one region (‘euchromatic’; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>) we observed one instance of copy number variation, five genes with premature stop codons, and seven RH-specific genes. These observations indicate that the plasticity of the potato genome is greater than revealed from the unassembled RH NGS. Improved assembly algorithms, increased read lengths, and <i>de novo</i> sequences of additional haplotypes will reveal the full catalogue of genes critical to inbreeding depression.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Tuber biology"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Tuber biology</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>In developing DM and RH tubers, 15,235 genes were expressed in the transition from stolons to tubers, with 1,217 transcripts exhibiting &gt;5-fold expression in stolons versus five RH tuber tissues (young tuber, mature tuber, tuber peel, cortex and pith; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 19</a>). Of these, 333 transcripts were upregulated during the transition from stolon to tuber, with the most highly upregulated transcripts encoding storage proteins. Foremost among these were the genes encoding proteinase inhibitors and patatin (15 genes), in which the phospholipase A function has been largely replaced by a protein storage function in the tuber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Prat, S. et al. Gene expression during tuber development in potato plants. FEBS Lett. 268, 334–338 (1990)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2468">15</a></sup>. In particular, a large family of 28 Kunitz protease inhibitor genes (KTIs) was identified with twice the number of genes in potato compared to tomato. The KTI genes are distributed across the genome with individual members exhibiting specific expression patterns (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig4">Fig. 4a, b</a>). KTIs are frequently induced after pest and pathogen attack and act primarily as inhibitors of exogenous proteinases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Glaczinski, H., Heibges, A., Salamini, R. &amp; Gebhardt, C. Members of the Kunitz-type protease inhibitor gene family of potato inhibit soluble tuber invertase in vitro . Potato Res. 45, 163–176 (2002)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2475">16</a></sup>; therefore the expansion of the KTI family may provide resistance to biotic stress for the newly evolved vulnerable underground organ.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="
                        Gene expression of selected tissues and genes.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: 
                        <b>Gene expression of selected tissues and genes.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10158/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10158_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1185"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, KTI gene organization across the potato genome. Black arrows indicate the location of individual genes on six scaffolds located on four chromosomes. <b>b</b>, Phylogenetic tree and KTI gene expression heat map. The KTI genes were clustered using all potato and tomato genes available with the <i>Populus</i> KTI gene as an out-group. The tissue specificity of individual members of the highly expanded potato gene family is shown in the heat map. Expression levels are indicated by shades of red, where white indicates no expression or lack of data for tomato and poplar. <b>c</b>, A model of starch synthesis showing enzyme activities is shown on the left. AGPase, ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase; F16BP, fructose-1,6-biphosphatase; HexK, hexokinase; INV, invertase; PFK, phosphofructokinase; PFPP, pyrophosphate-fructose-6-phosphate-1-phosphotransferase; PGI, phosphoglucose isomerase; PGM, phosphoglucomutase; SBE, starch branching enzyme; SP, starch phosphorylase; SPP, sucrose phosphate phosphatase; SS, starch synthase; SuSy, sucrose synthase; SUPS, sucrose phosphate synthase; UDP-GPP, UDP-glucose pyrophosphorylase. The grey background denotes substrate (sucrose) and product (starch) and the red background indicates genes that are specifically upregulated in RH versus DM. On the right, a heat map of the genes involved in carbohydrate metabolism is shown. ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase large subunit, AGPase (l); ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase small subunit, AGPase (s); ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase small subunit 3, AGPase 3 (s); cytosolic fructose-1,6-biphosphatase, F16BP (c); granule bound starch synthase, GBSS; leaf type L starch phosphorylase, Leaf type SP; plastidic phosphoglucomutase, pPGM; starch branching enzyme II, SBE II; soluble starch synthase, SSS; starch synthase V, SSV; three variants of plastidic aldolase, PA.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM257">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10158/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The stolon to tuber transition also coincides with strong upregulation of genes associated with starch biosynthesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). We observed several starch biosynthetic genes that were 3–8-fold more highly expressed in tuber tissues of RH compared to DM (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). Together this suggests a stronger shift from the relatively low sink strength of the ATP-generating general carbon metabolism reactions towards the plastidic starch synthesis pathway in tubers of RH, thereby causing a flux of carbon into the amyloplast. This contrasts with the cereal endosperm where carbon is transported into the amyloplast in the form of ADP-glucose via a specific transporter (brittle 1 protein<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shannon, J. C., Pien, F. M. &amp; Liu, K. C. Nucleotides and nucleotide sugars in developing maize endosperms: synthesis of ADP-glucose in brittle-1 . Plant Physiol. 110, 835–843 (1996)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2529">17</a></sup>). Carbon transport into the amyloplasts of potato tubers is primarily in the form of glucose-6-phosphate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Tauberger, E. et al. Antisense inhibition of plastidial phosphoglucomutase provides compelling evidence that potato tuber amyloplasts import carbon from the cytosol in the form of glucose-6-phosphate. Plant J. 23, 43–53 (2000)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2533">18</a></sup>, although recent evidence indicates that glucose-1-phosphate is quantitatively important under certain conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Fettke, J. et al. Glucose 1-phosphate is efficiently taken up by potato (Solanum tuberosum) tuber parenchyma cells and converted to reserve starch granules. New Phytol. 185, 663–675 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2537">19</a></sup>. The transport mechanism for glucose-1-phosphate is unknown and the genome sequence contains six genes for hexose-phosphate transporters with two highly and specifically expressed in stolons and tubers. Furthermore, an additional 23 genes encode proteins homologous to other carbohydrate derivative transporters, such as triose phosphate, phosphoenolpyruvate, or UDP-glucuronic acid transporters and two loci with homologues for the brittle 1 protein. By contrast, in leaves, carbon-fixation-specific genes such as plastidic aldolase, fructose-1,6-biphosphatase and distinct leaf isoforms of starch synthase, starch branching enzyme, starch phosphorylase and ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase were upregulated. Of particular interest is the difference in tuber expression of enzymes involved in the hydrolytic and phosphorolytic starch degradation pathways. Considerably greater levels of α-amylase (10–25-fold) and β-amylase (5–10-fold) mRNAs were found in DM tubers compared to RH, whereas α-1,4 glucan phosphorylase mRNA was equivalent in DM and RH tubers. These gene expression differences between the breeding line RH and the more primitive DM are consistent with the concept that increasing tuber yield may be partially attained by selection for decreased activity of the hydrolytic starch degradation pathway.</p><p>Recent studies using a potato genotype strictly dependent on short days for tuber induction (<i>S. tuberosum</i> group Andigena) identified a potato homologue (<i>SP6A</i>) of <i>A. thaliana FLOWERING LOCUS T</i> (<i>FT</i>) as the long-distance tuberization inductive signal. SP6A is produced in the leaves, consistent with its role as the mobile signal (S. Prat, personal communication). <i>SP/FT</i> is a multi-gene family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>) and expression of a second <i>FT</i> homologue, <i>SP5G</i>, in mature tubers suggests a possible function in the control of tuber sprouting, a photoperiod-dependent phenomenon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Sonnewald, U. Control of potato tuber sprouting. Trends Plant Sci. 6, 333–335 (2001)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2572">20</a></sup>. Likewise, expression of a homologue of the <i>A. thaliana</i> flowering time MADS box gene <i>SOC1</i>, acting downstream of <i>FT</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yoo, S. K. et al. CONSTANS activates SUPPRESSOR OF OVEREXPRESSION OF CONSTANS 1 through FLOWERING LOCUS T to promote flowering in Arabidopsis . Plant Physiol. 139, 770–778 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2585">21</a></sup>, is restricted to tuber sprouts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Expression of a third <i>FT</i> homologue, <i>SP3D</i>, does not correlate with tuberization induction but instead with transition to flowering, which is regulated independently of day length (S. Prat, personal communication). These data indicate that neofunctionalization of the day-length-dependent flowering control pathway has occurred in potato to control formation and possibly sprouting of a novel storage organ, the tuber (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Disease resistance"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Disease resistance</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Potato is susceptible to a wide range of pests and pathogens and the identification of genes conferring disease resistance has been a major focus of the research community. Most cloned disease resistance genes in the Solanaceae encode nucleotide-binding site (NBS) and leucine-rich-repeat (LRR) domains. The DM assembly contains 408 NBS-LRR-encoding genes, 57 Toll/interleukin-1 receptor/plant R gene homology (TIR) domains and 351 non-TIR types (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 20</a>), similar to the 402 resistance (<i>R</i>) gene candidates in <i>Populus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Kohler, A. et al. Genome-wide identi?cation of NBS resistance genes in Populus trichocarpa . Plant Mol. Biol. 66, 619–636 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2619">22</a></sup>. Highly related homologues of the cloned potato late blight resistance genes <i>R1</i>, <i>RB</i>, <i>R2</i>, <i>R3a</i>, <i>Rpi-blb2</i> and <i>Rpi-vnt1.1</i> were present in the assembly. In RH, the chromosome 5 R1 cluster contains two distinct haplotypes; one is collinear with the R1 region in DM (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>), yet neither the DM nor the RH R1 regions are collinear with other potato R1 regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ballvora, A. et al. Comparative sequence analysis of Solanum and Arabidopsis in a hot spot for pathogen resistance on potato chromosome V reveals a patchwork of conserved and rapidly evolving genome segments. BMC Genomics 8, 112 (2007)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2645">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kuang, H. et al. The R1 resistance gene cluster contains three groups of independently evolving, type I R1 homologues and shows substantial structural variation among haplotypes of Solanum demissum . Plant J. 44, 37–51 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2648">24</a></sup>. Comparison of the DM potato <i>R</i> gene sequences with well-established gene models (functional <i>R</i> genes) indicates that many NBS-LRR genes (39.4%) are pseudogenes owing to indels, frameshift mutations, or premature stop codons including the <i>R1</i>, <i>R3a</i> and <i>Rpi-vnt1.1</i> clusters that contain extensive chimaeras and exhibit evolutionary patterns of type I <i>R</i> genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kuang, H., Woo, S. S., Meyers, B. C., Nevo, E. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Multiple genetic processes result in heterogeneous rates of evolution within the major cluster disease resistance genes in lettuce. Plant Cell 16, 2870–2894 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2672">25</a></sup>. This high rate of pseudogenization parallels the rapid evolution of effector genes observed in the potato late blight pathogen, <i>Phytophthora infestans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Irish potato famine pathogen Phytophthora infestans . Nature 461, 393–398 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2678">26</a></sup>. Coupled with abundant haplotype diversity, tetraploid potato may therefore contain thousands of <i>R</i>-gene analogues.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Conclusions and future directions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Conclusions and future directions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>We sequenced a unique doubled-monoploid potato clone to overcome the problems associated with genome assembly due to high levels of heterozygosity and were able to generate a high-quality draft potato genome sequence that provides new insights into eudicot genome evolution. Using a combination of data from the vigorous, heterozygous diploid RH and relatively weak, doubled-monoploid DM, we could directly address the form and extent of heterozygosity in potato and provide the first view into the complexities that underlie inbreeding depression. Combined with other recent studies, the potato genome sequence may elucidate the evolution of tuberization. This evolutionary innovation evolved exclusively in the <i>Solanum</i> section <i>Petota</i> that encompasses <span class="stix">∼</span>200 species distributed from the southwestern United States to central Argentina and Chile. Neighbouring <i>Solanum</i> species, including the <i>Lycopersicon</i> section, which comprises wild and cultivated tomatoes, did not acquire this trait. Both gene family expansion and recruitment of existing genes for new pathways contributed to the evolution of tuber development in potato.</p><p>Given the pivotal role of potato in world food production and security, the potato genome provides a new resource for use in breeding. Many traits of interest to plant breeders are quantitative in nature and the genome sequence will simplify both their characterization and deployment in cultivars. Whereas much genetic research is conducted at the diploid level in potato, almost all potato cultivars are tetraploid and most breeding is conducted in tetraploid material. Hence, the development of experimental and computational methods for routine and informative high-resolution genetic characterization of polyploids remains an important goal for the realization of many of the potential benefits of the potato genome sequence.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>DM1-3 516 R44 (DM) resulted from chromosome doubling of a monoploid (1<i>n</i> = 1<i>x</i> = 12) derived by anther culture of a heterozygous diploid (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 24) <i>S. tuberosum</i> group Phureja clone (PI 225669)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Haynes, F. L. in Prospects for the Potato in the Developing World: an International Symposium on Key Problems and Potentials for Greater Use of the Potato in the Developing World (ed. French, E. R. ) 100–110 (International Potato Center (CIP), 1972)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2732">27</a></sup>. RH89-039-16 (RH) is a diploid clone derived from a cross between a <i>S. tuberosum</i> ‘dihaploid’ (SUH2293) and a diploid clone (BC1034) generated from a cross between two <i>S. tuberosum</i> × <i>S. tuberosum</i> group Phureja hybrids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="van Os, H. et al. Construction of a 10,000-marker ultradense genetic recombination map of potato: providing a framework for accelerated gene isolation and a genomewide physical map. Genetics 173, 1075–1087 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2745">28</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Sequence data from three platforms, Sanger, Roche 454 Pyrosequencing, and Illumina Sequencing-by-Synthesis, were used to assemble the DM genome using the SOAPdenovo assembly algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2753">4</a></sup>. The RH genotype was sequenced using shotgun sequencing of BACs and WGS in which reads were mapped to the DM reference assembly. Superscaffolds were anchored to the 12 linkage groups using a combination of <i>in silico</i> and genetic mapping data. Repeat sequences were identified through sequence similarity at the nucleotide and protein level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2760">29</a></sup>. Genes were annotated using a combined approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2764">30</a></sup> on the repeat masked genome with <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions, protein similarity and transcripts to build optimal gene models. Illumina RNA-Seq reads were mapped to the DM draft sequence using Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2771">31</a></sup> and expression levels from the representative transcript were determined using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2776">32</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Online Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Online Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">DM whole-genome shotgun sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Libraries were constructed from DM genomic DNA and sequenced on the Sanger, Illumina Genome Analyser 2 (GA2) and Roche 454 platforms using standard protocols (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Text</a>). A BAC library and three fosmid libraries were end sequenced using the Sanger platform. For the Illumina GA2 platform, we generated 70.6 Gb of 37–73 bp paired-end reads from 16 libraries with insert lengths of 200–811 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 21 and 22</a>). We also generated 18.7 Gb of Illumina mate-pair libraries (2, 5 and 10 kb insert size). In total, 7.2 Gb of 454 single-end data were generated and applied for gap filling to improve the assembly, of which 4.7 Gb (12,594,513 reads) were incorporated into the final assembly. For the 8 and 20 kb 454 paired-end reads, representing 0.7 and 1.0 Gb of raw data respectively, 90.7 Mb (511,254 reads) and 211 Mb (1,525,992 reads), respectively, were incorporated into the final assembly.</p><p>We generated a high-quality potato genome using the short read assembly software SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2801">4</a></sup> (Version 1014). We first assembled 69.4 Gb of GA2 paired-end short reads into contigs, which are sequence assemblies without gaps composed of overlapping reads. To increase the assembly accuracy, only 78.3% of the reads with high quality were considered. Then contigs were further linked into scaffolds by paired-end relationships (<span class="stix">∼</span>300 to <span class="stix">∼</span>550 bp insert size), mate-pair reads (2 to approximately 10 kb), fosmid ends (<span class="stix">∼</span>40 kb, 90,407 pairs of end sequences) and BAC ends (<span class="stix">∼</span>100 kb, 71,375 pairs of end sequences). We then filled gaps with the entire short-read data generated using Illumina GA2 reads. The primary contig <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> size (the contig length such that using equal or longer contigs produces half of the bases of the assembled genome) was 697 bp and increased to 1,318 kb after gap-filling (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 23 and 24</a>). When only the paired-end relationships were used in the assembly process, the <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> scaffold size was 22.4 kb. Adding mate-pair reads with 2, 5 and 10 kb insert sizes, the <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> scaffold size increased to 67, 173 and 389 kb, respectively. When integrated with additional libraries of larger insert size, such as fosmid and BAC end sequences, the <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> reached 1,318 kb. The final assembly size was 727 Mb, 93.87% of which is non-gapped sequence. We further filled the gaps with 6.74 fold coverage of 454 data, which increased the <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> contig size to 31,429 bp with 15.4% of the gaps filled.</p><p>The single-base accuracy of the assembly was estimated by the depth and proportion of disconcordant reads. For the DM v3.0 assembly, 95.45% of 880 million usable reads could be mapped back to the assembled genome by SOAP 2.20 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: an improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966–1967 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2831">34</a>) using optimal parameters. The read depth was calculated for each genomic location and peak depth for whole genome and the CDS regions are 100 and 105, respectively. Approximately 96% of the assembled sequences had more than 20-fold coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). The overall GC content of the potato genome is about 34.8% with a positive correlation between GC content and sequencing depth (data not shown). The DM potato should have few heterozygous sites and 93.04% of the sites can be supported by at least 90% reads, suggesting high base quality and accuracy.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">RH genome sequencing</h3><p>Whole-genome sequencing of genotype RH was performed on the Illumina GA2 platform using a variety of fragment sizes and reads lengths resulting in a total of 144 Gb of raw data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 25</a>). These data were filtered using a custom C program and assembled using SOAPdenovo 1.03 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2848">4</a>). Additionally, four 20-kb mate-pair libraries were sequenced on a Roche 454 Titanium sequencer, amounting to 581 Mb of raw data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 26</a>). The resulting sequences were filtered for duplicates using custom Python scripts.</p><p>The RH BACs were sequenced using a combination of Sanger and 454 sequencing at various levels of coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 9–11</a>). Consensus base calling errors in the BAC sequences were corrected using custom Python and C scripts using a similar approach to that described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Chaisson, M., Pevzner, P. &amp; Tang, H. Fragment assembly with short reads. Bioinformatics 20, 2067–2074 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2861">35</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Text</a>). Sequence overlaps between BACs within the same physical tiling path were identified using megablast from BLAST 2.2.21 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2867">36</a>) and merged with megamerger from the EMBOSS 6.1.0 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2871">37</a></sup>. Using the same pipeline, several kilobase-sized gaps were closed through alignment of a preliminary RH whole-genome assembly. The resulting non-redundant contigs were scaffolded by mapping the RH whole-genome Illumina and 454 mated sequences against these contigs using SOAPalign 2.20 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: an improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966–1967 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2875">34</a>) and subsequently processing these mapping results with a custom Python script. The scaffolds were then ordered into superscaffolds based on the BAC order in the tiling paths of the FPC map. This procedure removed 25 Mb of redundant sequence, reduced the number of sequence fragments from 17,228 to 3,768, and increased the N50 sequence length from 24 to 144 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Tables 9 and 10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Construction of the DM genetic map and anchoring of the genome</h3><p>To anchor and fully orientate physical contigs along the chromosome, a genetic map was developed <i>de novo</i> using sequence-tagged-site (STS) markers comprising simple sequence repeats (SSR), SNPs, and diversity array technology (DArT). SSR and SNP markers were designed directly from assembled sequence scaffolds, whereas polymorphic DArT marker sequences were searched against the scaffolds for high-quality unique matches. A total of 4,836 STS markers including 2,174 DArTs, 2,304 SNPs and 358 SSRs were analysed on 180 progeny clones from a backcross population ((DM × DI) × DI) developed at CIP between DM and DI (CIP no. 703825), a heterozygous diploid <i>S. tuberosum</i> group Stenotomum (formerly <i>S. stenotomum</i> ssp. <i>goniocalyx</i>) landrace clone. The data from 2,603 polymorphic STS markers comprising 1,881 DArTs, 393 SNPs and 329 SSR alleles were analysed using JoinMap 4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Van Ooijen, J. W. in JoinMap 4, Software for the Calculation of Genetic Linkage Maps in Experimental Populations (ed. Kyazma, B. V. ) (Wageningen, 2006)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2901">38</a>) and yielded the expected 12 potato linkage groups. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 3</a> represents the mapping and anchoring of the potato genome, using chromosome 7 as an example.</p><p>Anchoring the DM genome was accomplished using direct and indirect approaches. The direct approach employed the ((DM × DI) × DI) linkage map whereby 2,037 of the 2,603 STS markers comprised of 1,402 DArTs, 376 SNPs and 259 SSRs could be uniquely anchored on the DM superscaffolds. This approach anchored <span class="stix">∼</span>52% (394 Mb) of the assembly arranged into 334 superscaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 27</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>RH is the male parent of the mapping population of the ultra-high-density (UHD) linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="van Os, H. et al. Construction of a 10,000-marker ultradense genetic recombination map of potato: providing a framework for accelerated gene isolation and a genomewide physical map. Genetics 173, 1075–1087 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2921">28</a></sup> used for construction and genetic anchoring of the physical map using the RHPOTKEY BAC library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Borm, T. J. Construction and Use of a Physical Map of Potato. PhD thesis, Wageningen Univ. (2008)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2925">39</a></sup>. The indirect mapping approach exploited <i>in silico</i> anchoring using the RH genetic and physical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="van Os, H. et al. Construction of a 10,000-marker ultradense genetic recombination map of potato: providing a framework for accelerated gene isolation and a genomewide physical map. Genetics 173, 1075–1087 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2932">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Visser, R. G. F. et al. Sequencing the potato genome: outline and first results to come from the elucidation of the sequence of the world's third most important crop. Am. J. Potato Res. 86, 417–429 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2935">40</a></sup>, as well as tomato genetic map data from SGN (<a href="http://solgenomics.net/">http://solgenomics.net/</a>).
