Amplified fragment length polymorphism markers from the RH genetic map were linked to DM sequence scaffolds via BLAST alignment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2947">36</a></sup> of whole-genome-profiling sequence tags<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Van der Vossen, E. et al. in Whole Genome Profiling of the Diploid Potato Clone RH89-039-16 (Plant &amp; Animal Genomes XVIII Conference, 2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2951">41</a></sup> obtained from anchored seed BACs in the RH physical map, or by direct alignment of fully sequenced RH seed BACs to the DM sequence. The combined marker alignments were processed into robust anchor points. The tomato sequence markers from the genetic maps were aligned to the DM assembly using SSAHA2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ning, Z., Cox, A. J. &amp; Mullikin, J. C. SSAHA: a fast search method for large DNA databases. Genome Res. 11, 1725–1729 (2001)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2954">42</a>). Positions of ambiguously anchored superscaffolds were manually checked and corrected. This approach anchored an additional <span class="stix">∼</span>32% of the assembly (229 Mb). In 294 cases, the two independent approaches provided direct support for each other, anchoring the same scaffold to the same position on the two maps.</p><p>Overall, the two strategies anchored 649 superscaffolds to approximate positions on the genetic map of potato covering a length of 623 Mb. The 623 Mb (<span class="stix">∼</span>86%) anchored genome includes <span class="stix">∼</span>90% of the 39,031 predicted genes. Of the unanchored superscaffolds, 84 were found in the N90 (622 scaffolds greater than 0.25 Mb), constituting 17 Mb of the overall assembly or 2% of the assembled genome. The longest anchored superscaffold is 7 Mb (from chromosome 1) and the longest unanchored superscaffold is 2.5 Mb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Identification of repetitive sequences</h3><p>Transposable elements (TEs) in the potato genome assembly were identified at the DNA and protein level. RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2969">29</a></sup> was applied using Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2973">43</a></sup> for TE identification at the DNA level. At the protein level, RepeatProteinMask<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2977">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Jiang, Z., Hubley, R., Smit, A. &amp; Eichler, E. E. DupMasker: a tool for annotating primate segmental duplications. Genome Res. 18, 1362–1368 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2980">44</a></sup> was used in a WuBlastX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d26978e2984">36</a></sup> search against the TE protein database to further identify TEs. Overlapping TEs belonging to the same repeat class were collated, and sequences were removed if they overlapped &gt;80% and belonged to different repeat classes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene prediction</h3><p>To predict genes, we performed <i>ab initio</i> predictions on the repeat-masked genome and then integrated the results with spliced alignments of proteins and transcripts to genome sequences using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3000">30</a></sup>. The potato genome was masked by identified repeat sequences longer than 500 bp, except for miniature inverted repeat transposable elements which are usually found near genes or inside introns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Kuang, H. et al. Identification of miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs) and biogenesis of their siRNAs in the Solanaceae: new functional implications for MITEs. Genome Res. 19, 42–56 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3004">45</a></sup>. The software Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309–W312 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3008">46</a></sup> and Genscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78–94 (1997)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3012">47</a></sup> was used for <i>ab initio</i> predictions with parameters trained for <i>A. thaliana</i>. For similarity-based gene prediction, we aligned the protein sequences of four sequenced plants (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>) onto the potato genome using TBLASTN with an <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, and then similar genome sequences were aligned against the matching proteins using Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3041">48</a></sup> for accurately spliced alignments. In EST-based predictions, EST sequences of 11 <i>Solanum</i> species were aligned against the potato genome using BLAT (identity ≥0.95, coverage ≥0.90) to generate spliced alignments. All these resources and prediction approaches were combined by GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3048">30</a></sup> to build the consensus gene set. To finalize the gene set, we aligned the RNA-Seq from 32 libraries, of which eight were sequenced with both single- and paired-end reads, to the genome using Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3052">31</a></sup> and the alignments were then used as input for Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3057">32</a></sup> using the default parameters. Gene, transcript and peptide sets were filtered to remove small genes, genes modelled across sequencing gaps, TE-encoding genes, and other incorrect annotations. The final gene set contains 39,031 genes with 56,218 protein-coding transcripts, of which 52,925 nonidentical proteins were retained for analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>RNA was isolated from many tissues of DM and RH that represent developmental, abiotic stress and biotic stress conditions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Text</a>). cDNA libraries were constructed (Illumina) and sequenced on an Illumina GA2 in the single- and/or paired-end mode. To represent the expression of each gene, we selected a representative transcript from each gene model by selecting the longest CDS from each gene. The aligned read data were generated by Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3075">31</a></sup> and the selected transcripts used as input into Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3079">32</a></sup>, a short-read transcript assembler that calculates the fragments per kb per million mapped reads (FPKM) as expression values for each transcript. Cufflinks was run with default settings, with a maximum intron length of 15,000. FPKM values were reported and tabulated for each transcript (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Comparative genome analyses</h3><p>Paralogous and orthologous clusters were identified using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Chen, F., Mackey, A. J., Vermunt, J. K. &amp; Roos, D. S. Assessing performance of orthology detection strategies applied to eukaryotic genomes. PLoS ONE 2, e383 (2007)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3094">49</a></sup> using the predicted proteomes of 11 plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 28</a>). After removing 1,602 TE-related genes that were not filtered in earlier annotation steps, asterid-specific and potato-lineage-specific genes were identified using the initial OrthoMCL clustering followed by BLAST searches (<i>E</i>-value cut-off of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) against assemblies of ESTs available from the PlantGDB project (<a href="http://plantgdb.org">http://plantgdb.org</a>; 153 nonasterid species and 57 asterid species; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 29</a>). Analysis of protein domains was performed using the Pfam hmm models identified by InterProScan searches against InterPro (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro</a>). We compared the Pfam domains of the asterid-specific and potato-lineage-specific sets with those that are shared with at least one other nonasterid genome or transcriptome. A Fisher’s exact test was used to detect significant differences in Pfam representation between protein sets.</p><p>After removing the self and multiple matches, the syntenic blocks (≥5 genes per block) were identified using MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3130">9</a></sup> and i-adhore 3.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Simillion, C., Janssens, K., Sterck, L. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. i-ADHoRe 2.0: an improved tool to detect degenerated genomic homology using genomic profiles. Bioinformatics 24, 127–128 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3133">50</a>) based on the aligned protein gene pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10158#MOESM258">Supplementary Table 8</a>). For the self-aligned results, each aligned block represents the paralogous segments pair that arose from the genome duplication whereas, for the inter-species alignment results, each aligned block represents the orthologous pair derived from the shared ancestor. We calculated the 4DTv (fourfold degenerate synonymous sites of the third codons) for each gene pair from the aligned block and give a distribution for the 4DTv value to estimate the speciation or WGD event that occurred in evolutionary history.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Identification of disease resistance genes</h3><p>Predicted open reading frames (ORFs) from the annotation of <i>S. tuberosum</i> group Phureja assembly V3 were screened using HMMER V.3 (<a href="http://hmmer.janelia.org/software">http://hmmer.janelia.org/software</a>) against the raw hidden Markov model (HMM) corresponding to the Pfam NBS (NB-ARC) family (PF00931). The HMM was downloaded from the Pfam home page (<a href="http://pfam.sanger.ac.uk/">http://pfam.sanger.ac.uk/</a>). The analysis using the raw HMM of the NBS domain resulted in 351 candidates. From these, a high quality protein set (&lt;1 × 10<sup>−60</sup>) was aligned and used to construct a potato-specific NBS HMM using the module ‘hmmbuild’. Using this new potato-specific model, we identified 500 NBS-candidate proteins that were individually analysed. To detect TIR and LRR domains, Pfam HMM searches were used. The raw TIR HMM (PF01582) and LRR 1 HMM (PF00560) were downloaded and compared against the two sets of NBS-encoding amino acid sequences using HMMER V3. Both TIR and LRR domains were validated using NCBI conserved domains and multiple expectation maximization for motif elicitation (MEME)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Bailey, T. L. &amp; Elkan, C. The value of prior knowledge in discovering motifs with MEME. Proc. Int. Conf. Intell. Syst. Mol. Biol. 3, 21–29 (1995)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3167">51</a></sup>. In the case of LRRs, MEME was also useful to detect the number of repeats of this particular domain in the protein. As previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Mun, J. H., Yu, H. J., Park, S. &amp; Park, B. S. Genome-wide identification of NBS-encoding resistance genes in Brassica rapa . Mol. Genet. Genomics 282, 617–631 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3172">52</a></sup>, Pfam analysis could not identify the CC motif in the N-terminal region. CC domains were thus analysed using the MARCOIL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Delorenzi, M. &amp; Speed, T. An HMM model for coiled-coil domains and a comparison with PSSM-based predictions. Bioinformatics 18, 617–625 (2002)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3176">53</a></sup> program with a threshold probability of 90 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Mun, J. H., Yu, H. J., Park, S. &amp; Park, B. S. Genome-wide identification of NBS-encoding resistance genes in Brassica rapa . Mol. Genet. Genomics 282, 617–631 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3179">52</a>) and double-checked using paircoil2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="McDonnell, A. V., Jiang, T., Keating, A. E. &amp; Berger, B. Paircoil2: improved predictions of coiled coils from sequence. Bioinformatics 22, 356–358 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3182">54</a>) with a <i>P-</i>score cut-off of 0.025 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Porter, B. W. et al. Genome-wide analysis of Carica papaya reveals a small NBS resistance gene family. Mol. Genet. Genomics 281, 609–626 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3188">55</a>). Selected genes (±1.5 kb) were searched using BLASTX against a reference <i>R</i>-gene set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Sanseverino, W. et al. PRGdb: a bioinformatics platform for plant resistance gene analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D814–D821 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3196">56</a></sup> to find a well-characterized homologue. The reference set was used to select and annotate as pseudogenes those peptides that had large deletions, insertions, frameshift mutations, or premature stop codons. DNA and protein comparisons were used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Haplotype diversity analysis</h3><p>RH reads generated by the Illumina GA2 were mapped onto the DM genome assembly using SOAP2.20 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: an improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966–1967 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3207">34</a>) allowing at most four mismatches and SNPs were called using SOAPsnp. Q20 was used to filter the SNPs owing to sequencing errors. To exclude SNP calling errors caused by incorrect alignments, we excluded adjacent SNPs separated by &lt;5 bp. SOAPindel was used to detect the indels between DM and RH. Only indels supported by more than three uniquely mapped reads were retained. Owing to the heterozygosity of RH, the SNPs and indels were classified into heterozygous and homozygous SNPs or indels.</p><p>On the basis of the annotated genes in the DM genome assembly, we extracted the SNPs located at coding regions and stop codons. If a homozygous SNP in RH within a coding region induced a premature stop codon, we defined the gene harbouring this SNP as a homozygous premature stop gene in RH. If the SNP inducing a premature stop codon was heterozygous, the gene harbouring this SNP was considered a heterozygous premature stop codon gene in RH. In addition, both categories can be further divided into premature stop codons shared with DM or not shared with DM. As a result, the numbers of premature stop codons are 606 homozygous PS genes in RH, 1,760 heterozygous PS genes in RH but not shared with DM, 288 PS in DM only, and 652 heterozygous premature stop codons in RH and shared by DM.</p><p>To identify genes with frameshift mutations in RH, we identified all the genes containing indels of which the length could not be divided by 3. We found 80 genes with frameshift mutations, of which 31 were heterozygous and 49 were homozygous.</p><p>To identify DM-specific genes, we mapped all the RH Illumina GA2 reads to the DM genome assembly. If the gene was not mapped to any RH read, it was considered a DM-specific gene. We identified 35 DM-specific genes, 11 of which are supported by similarity to entries in the KEGG database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Kawashima, S., Okuno, Y. &amp; Hattori, M. The KEGG resource for deciphering the genome. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D277–D280 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3220">57</a></sup>. To identify RH-specific genes, we assembled the RH Illumina GA2 reads that did not map to the DM genome into RH-specific scaffolds. Then, these scaffolds were annotated using the same strategy as for DM. To exclude contamination, we aligned the CDS sequences against the protein set of bacteria with the <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup> using Blastx. CDS sequences with &gt;90% identity and &gt;90% coverage were considered contaminants and were excluded. In addition, all DM RNA-seq reads were mapped onto the CDS sequences, and CDS sequences with homologous reads were excluded because these genes may be due to incorrect assembly. In total, we predicted 246 RH specific genes, 34 of which are supported by Gene Ontology annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shannon, J. C., Pien, F. M. &amp; Liu, K. C. Nucleotides and nucleotide sugars in developing maize endosperms: synthesis of ADP-glucose in brittle-1 . Plant Physiol. 110, 835–843 (1996)" href="/articles/nature10158#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d26978e3229">17</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Data deposits</h3>
                <p>BAC and fosmid end sequences have been deposited in the GSS division of GenBank (BAC: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GS025503%96GS026177">GS025503–GS026177</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GS262924%96GS365942">GS262924–GS365942</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GS504213%96GS557003">GS504213–GS557003</a>; fosmid: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=FI900795%96FI901529">FI900795–FI901529</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=FI907952%96FI927051">FI907952–FI927051</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GS557234%96GS594339">GS557234–GS594339</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GS635316%96GS765761">GS635316–GS765761</a>). DM Illumina GA2 WGS and Roche 454 sequences have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA029323">SRA029323</a>) and EBI Short Read Archive (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=ERP000411">ERP000411</a>) respectively. RH NGS sequences have been deposited in the EBI Short Read Archive (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=ERP000627">ERP000627</a>). DM and RH RNA-Seq reads have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA030516">SRA030516</a>; study <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP005965">SRP005965</a>) and the European Nucleotide Database ArrayExpress Database (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/microarray-as/aer/result?queryFor=Experiment&amp;eAccession=E-MTAB-552">E-MTAB-552</a>; study <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=ERP000527">ERP000527</a>), respectively. The DM Whole Genome Shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession AEWC00000000. The version described in this paper is the First Version, AEWC01000000. Genome sequence and annotation can be obtained and viewed at <a href="http://potatogenome.net">http://potatogenome.net</a>.</p>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">The potato genome</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The genome of the potato (<i>Solanum tuberosum</i> L.), a staple crop vital to food security, has been sequenced. The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium sequenced a homozygous doubled-monoploid potato clone as well as a heterozygous diploid clone. Genome analysis reveals traces of at least two genome duplication events and genes specific to Asterids, a large clade of flowering plants of which the potato is the first to be sequenced. Gene presence/absence variants and other potentially deleterious mutations are frequent and may be the cause of inbreeding depression. The genome sequence will facilitate genetic improvements in the potato with a view to improving yield and to increasing disease and stress resistance of this crop, which is a now a significant component of worldwide food production and is becoming increasingly important in the developing world.</p></div></section></div></aside>
